## Thread: ORACLE takes over Sun (rest of us may have to live in darkness...) — 2 this month / 12 total

_Topics: [`Databases and SQL`](topics.md#databases) · [`Web, XML, modern integration`](topics.md#web)_

_Thread started [2009-04](2009-04.md#t-a2c9039b19)._

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: ORACLE takes over Sun (rest of us may have to live in darkness...)

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-01T00:58:11+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75tlj5F1a574vU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<75lkciFtcsqpU1@mid.individual.net> <770d2f29-05cd-45d0-a1c3-6c6f583b295d@g31g2000pra.googlegroups.com> <75slbdF19ljj6U1@mid.individual.net> <OBgKl.15476$hc1.4551@flpi150.ffdc.sbc.com>`

```
Michael Mattias wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:75slbdF19ljj6U1@mid.individual.net...
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> over fifty years - and continue to do so.

Absolutely.  Like your car, it can be argued that it "doesn't owe you 
anything."

But, also like the car, it is still useful and therefore still has value. 
However, if the cost of maintaining your car became more than actually 
buying a new one, it is likely that you would visit the showroom...

Unfortunately, many sites are deciding that COBOL is just not a viable 
option (sometimes assisted by ERP package vendors...) and they are right 
that it isn't, if you plan to extend your existing systems into the future. 
These sites decide to "write off" their existing COBOL (whether it owes them 
anything or not) and consider the installation of "new technology" as a 
perfect excuse for dropping it and telling the accountants that the bullet 
had to be bitten.... My comment was aimed at this "write off" which I think 
is largely unnecessary, as COBOL CAN be refactored and salvaged.(I know it 
can because I've been doing it for some time now... :-))

Consider the scenario where it is considered desirable to modernize the 
corporate systems. .NET is the desired target, the Web will be the 
transport, and existing legacy systems will be gradually replaced by C# and 
or Java and scripting languages. Most people don't see COBOL having a place 
in this environment, but I do.

Depending on the age and maintenance levels of the existing legacy code, it 
may be possible to salvage over 85% of the existing functionality and STILL 
move to the specified target environment. COBOL CAN play nicely with new 
technology; it just needs some "protective clothing" on it.

Wrapping COBOL as OO components is easy and it is worth doing.

When I moved to C# I was able to transport around 80% of my existing COBOL 
legacy. I didn't expect it, but it was a considerable saving. Everything 
works seamlessly and components don't care whether they are written in C# or 
COBOL, or even scripted as WSH, VBScript, or Javascript.

 The .NET framework simply rocks...
>
> I'd say overall, COBOL-based applications have provided a pretty
> decent return on the investment; they have paid their own way and
> then some.

You won't hear any disagreement about that from me. But, you have to be fair 
about the cost of maintaining them.

I estimate my productivity has doubled since I dropped COBOL. A fair amount 
of that is because I can fix things in half the time it used to take. (I'd 
like to think I'm getting smarter :-), but I realise that is very unlikely, 
and it is really down to better tools, more powerful language, and instant 
support on tap 24/7.

>
> Kind of like the 1996 Mercury Grand Marquis I bought new. It's no
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>
But if you were told the transmission needed replacing, the big end bearings 
were going, the diff needed a new crown wheel and the whole lot was going to 
be thousands of dollars, you'd probably opt to cut your losses and put the 
money to a deposit on a newer car, right?

Pete.
```

Yes, that is a logical approach which most people would agree with.

(I have to 'fess up to NOT doing this, but purely for illogical and 
emotional reasons. I still have the car I bought 19 years ago. I don't let 
mechanics touch it and do all the routine maintenance myself. I have driven 
it 120,000 miles and it still purrs like a sewing machine. It looks like it 
just came out of the showroom (I care for it lovingly), passes all its 
regular safety checks with flying colours and is a source of constant 
amusement and delight to me. From time to time I think about replacing it 
but then I just shudder and think about something else... For, many people, 
(self included), our love affair with cars is completely irrational, but 
real, nonetheless...)

…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> have?'

Yes, I have encountered this too. It is a two-edged sword...

"Hey, Boss, wanna save $12,000,000?  Instead of spending 15 million to 
rewrite everything so it can run in .NET, why don't we re-wrap what we have 
so our existing system can run in .NET (with other languages, if you like)? 
Didn't know you could do that? You gotta stop reading those airline 
magazines and talk with us more..."

Pete.

## Thread: Questionnaire for academic research on the programming language COBOL — 2 this month / 3 total

_Thread started [2009-04](2009-04.md#t-ec1339357b)._

#### ↳ Re: Questionnaire for academic research on the programming language COBOL

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-01T15:28:34+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75v8j1F193plhU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<5196fa60-0317-493d-8781-9ad10a3c515d@r31g2000prh.googlegroups.com>`

```
Anthony Aquino wrote:
> Hi guys, how are you?
>
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> Anthony.

I did the questionnaire but some of the questions are not immediately 
obvious. (I think the translation may be "off").

Also, the questionnaiure is too black and white.

As an example. "Does COBOL document itself well?"

It depends entirely on who's writing it.

WELL WRITTEN COBOL is almost self-documenting, but POORLY WRITTEN COBOL is 
not.
...
01 ztfb pic s9(4) comp sync right.
01 fltctr redefines ztfb pic xx.
...

if fltctr less than or = 12 perform zgtlwx varying ztfb from fltctr until 
ztfb > 20 or fltctr unequal fltctr

and so on ad nauseum...

Good luck with your studies.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-63efca4eb6"></a>
## Thread: COBDATA Tool and Web site — 18 msgs

_Topics: [`Web, XML, modern integration`](topics.md#web)_

### COBDATA Tool and Web site

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-01T16:30:52+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<75vc7qF1ag0rgU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
I am extremely pleased to announce that the COBDATA site will be back on the 
air from Monday, officially, but bits of it will be appearing over the 
weekend.

The site has been completely revised and extended to cover more areas of 
COBOL interest. You will find:

1. Free downloads of tools, components, source code, scripts, and tutorials.
2. Information about how COBOL investment can be recovered. Full examples of 
doing it.
3. A portal to Open COBOL.
4. Background and conceptual stuff on OO, components, and mixing languages.
5. Using Relational databases with COBOL. Converting flat files to 
Relational Database, the traps and pitfalls and how to avoid and overcome 
them.
6. A complete technical strategy for migrating procedural COBOL to the .NET 
or Mono framework. Details on how to do it manually; overview of tools 
available from PRIMA if you want help.
7. There is a bit of nostalgia and some stuff that may bring a tear to the 
eye of old time mainframers... :-) There is stuff you can mess with, 
interact with, and play with.

I have gone to considerable trouble to ensure the site renders properly in 
Firefox and IE browsers, but Bill Klein pointed out an important thing that 
is lacking:  DPI values for the vision impaired. If your display is set to 
values other than 96 DPI, pages will not render correctly. I can only 
apologise for this. It is simply too hard to fix (properly) at the moment. 
However, I am investigating the arrival of VSG fonts and when I can do 
something about it, I will.

If you want to contribute to the site, have code samples or experiences you 
want to share, please contact me. I have deliberately NOT  put a forum on 
the site because I'd like the forum for it to be this one. The site is 
intended to be a resource for COBOL people (and programmers from other 
disciplines who have to deal with COBOL legacy), and guides site visitors 
back to this forum. Issues and questions arising from material on the site 
can be discussed here for the benefit of all, and discussions here can lead 
to things being posted on the site (this has already happened...)

There is no advertising on the site and there is unlikely to be. PRIMA 
Computing (my company) sponsors the site and covers the server costs, but I 
have insisted that the PRIMA presence be very low key (there is one indirect 
page that describes the PRIMA toolset and it is a technical page, not a 
marketing one). You will not be assaulted with special offers, or demands to 
try PRIMA products. Neither is your registration added to a marketing 
database. If you register, you can remove yourself any time you want to. 
Registered users have full access (including delete) to their DB entries. (I 
think ALL web sites should be like this...)

The site is safe and I promise you it is safe to enable cookies and flash 
for this site. Cookies expire after 10 minutes or 24 hours, depending on the 
context they are raised in. NOTHING is ever placed on your computer unless 
you request a download. Downloads are thoroughly malware checked
 and I wouldn't offer them unless I was satisfied they are clean.

Enjoy the site and post any comments or questions back here.

http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobdata

Pete.
```

I hate to admit how naive I am, but up until about 1985 I thought CICS 
assembler had to be macro-level and only CICS COBOL could be 
command-level.  That was just the way it was done at that VSE & VM shop.

It wasn't until I went to work for my current employer in 1987 that I 
encountered macro-level CICS COBOL, and it was somewhat challenging. 
That was an MVS shop (now z/OS) and the application supported a mixture 
of macro-level and command-level CICS COBOL.  As I have said before, IBM 
was telling us in 1985 that macro-level was on the way out and 
eventually only command level CICS would supported.  I haven't even seen 
a macro-level CICS program since 1992.

One of my fond memories from 1979 is the first paid programming 
assignment I ever had.  It was converting an assembler day-of-week 
subroutine from IBM 360 Assembler to IBM Series/1 ANSI-74 COBOL.  It 
used Zeller's congruence (though I didn't know it was called that at the 
time) and I managed to get it working without understanding the formula. 
  We also discovered a compiler bug and I wrote my first (and so far 
only)  official bug report to IBM.

I think it would actually be fun to rewrite assembler programs into 
COBOL.  I have done it a few times before, though never as line-by-line 
translation.  If it's functionally equivalent that ought to be good 
enough for anyone.

With kindest regards,
```

On Fri, 1 May 2009 12:52:57 -0400, "Kerry Liles"

>

To tell you the truth, that's a job I'd relish.  And if you are really
converting line by line, take it from me as someone who has already
done this exercie, that is the wrong approach to take.  The better
approach is to analyze what the program is supposed to accomplish,
given the input and the desired output, and write Cobol code to do
this.  You would certainly consult the Assembler code to discern
possible status byte tests, or condition code tests or field vaules
and paths to take, but going line by line will result in very ugly
Cobol code.

Regards,

         (o o)

companies with commercial support options [1] most are not but still in
widespread use.

Source Software / Free Software packages. OpenCOBOL not (yet) being one

## Thread: Acucobol printing with VISTA — 5 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### Acucobol printing with VISTA

- **From:** Gio <giovanni_dimaio@virgilio.it>
- **Date:** 2009-05-02T11:37:37-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<52659c1b-cdd1-433d-be93-bf85823afd14@s28g2000vbp.googlegroups.com>`

```
Hi,
in my ACUCOBOL (4.3) programs I use to print my reports using the
configuration option
PRINTER -P SPOOLER-DIRECT

I normally open a file using the port name or the netwoork address of
the printer

open output LPT1
or
open output \\server\hplj4200

All this worked fine with WindowsXP and Server2003 but I'm facing a
big problem using Windows Vista.
I cannot print on networked printers.
I tried
net use LPT1 \\comp_name\Printer_name
without any success.

The software works only if I have a printer fisically connected to the
port.

Did someone have any suggestion ?

Thanks

Giovanni.
```

#### ↳ Re: Acucobol printing with VISTA

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-03T12:56:11+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7648d3F1aqgj7U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<52659c1b-cdd1-433d-be93-bf85823afd14@s28g2000vbp.googlegroups.com>`

```
Gio wrote:
> Hi,
> in my ACUCOBOL (4.3) programs I use to print my reports using the
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>

One way to solve this is to install a "print-to-file" printer driver, then 
select that as the printer.

Install the driver for the printer you currently use, using add a printer in 
Control Panel.  When the wizard asks what port to use say: "FILE:"

Give this copy of the driver a different printer name in your printer 
selection options and then just use that "printer" when you want to print to 
file, and the real printer when you want hard copy.

HTH,

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Acucobol printing with VISTA

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-05-04T09:20:44-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gtmv1n0252k@news7.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<52659c1b-cdd1-433d-be93-bf85823afd14@s28g2000vbp.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<52659c1b-cdd1-433d-be93-bf85823afd14@s28g2000vbp.googlegroups.com>`

```
Gio wrote:
> 
> All this worked fine with WindowsXP and Server2003 but I'm facing a
> big problem using Windows Vista.
> I cannot print on networked printers.

I see you've already received some suggestions which may resolve this
for you, but if not:

- Are you running your applications normally, from the desktop?
They're not running as Windows services or anything like that, are
they? Vista (and Server 2008) did significantly improve the separation
between processes running in different contexts, and this has broken a
number of programs that worked (but were security holes) in earlier
versions of Windows.

- Vista apparently includes several mysterious changes to printing
support. I've seen a handful of customer printing issues with AcuCOBOL
on Vista, and they're often in code that's entirely outside Acu - in
the Windows printing API, system dialogs, drivers, etc. (For example,
configuring multiple copies in the Windows printer driver dialog
apparently no longer works on Vista; the option is there, but setting
it has no effect.) These are Windows bugs, and there's nothing we can
do about them. This doesn't sound like that sort of problem, but it's
difficult to tell.

- Have you tried printing to the network printer from another program?
That seems obvious, but you didn't say so in your original note, so I
thought I should ask.

- Have you contacted MF support with this issue?
```

---

<a id="t-063356e4c1"></a>
## Thread: .CVS to .XLS — 15 msgs

### .CVS to .XLS

- **From:** "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net>
- **Date:** 2009-05-02T16:55:20-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<49fcc158$0$23780$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Per my post named "XLS, not .CSV from NETX?" dated Tuesday, March 24, 2009 
1:16 PM I have solved my problem by having the COBOL program create a .CSV 
file, then calling a program that reads the .CSV file and creates a .XLS 
file, with the headings bold, the columns sized correctly, etc.  It uses the 
invoke command extensively.  I need one that will cause a field that is 
expressed as .1234 to display as 12.34% - does one exist?   TIA, Paul
```

#### ↳ Re: .CVS to .XLS

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-03T13:05:51+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7648v7F1ak1i8U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49fcc158$0$23780$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul H wrote:
> Per my post named "XLS, not .CSV from NETX?" dated Tuesday, March 24,
> 2009 1:16 PM I have solved my problem by having the COBOL program
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> 12.34% - does one exist?   TIA, Paul

Sure, add another invoke to the program you have. Invoke the cell properties 
of the cell that will have the number in it, so that it formats the string 
how you want for that cell. Have a look at how the program gets the headings 
to be bold, and follow that principle. Excel cells have properties (they are 
just objects...) and those properties can be manipulated through Automation 
as you are seeing.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: .CVS to .XLS

- **From:** "Paul H" <NoSpamphobergNoSpam@att.net>
- **Date:** 2009-05-02T23:37:40-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:7648v7F1ak1i8U1@mid.individual.net...
Paul H wrote:
Per my post named "XLS, not .CSV from NETX?" dated Tuesday, March 24,
2009 1:16 PM I have solved my problem by having the COBOL program
create a .CSV file, then calling a program that reads the .CSV file
and creates a .XLS file, with the headings bold, the columns sized
correctly, etc.  It uses the invoke command extensively.  I need one
that will cause a field that is expressed as .1234 to display as
12.34% - does one exist?   TIA, Paul

Sure, add another invoke to the program you have. Invoke the cell properties
of the cell that will have the number in it, so that it formats the string
how you want for that cell. Have a look at how the program gets the headings
to be bold, and follow that principle. Excel cells have properties (they are
just objects...) and those properties can be manipulated through Automation
as you are seeing.

Pete.

If you create a blank Excel spreadsheet on your system then modify the 
cells, headings, etc. to look how you want, you will learn a few things 
about Excel. Use the VBA Help, as Frederico suggested to see the properties 
you can change. The point is that if you do it "manually" you don't need to 
worry about the programming interface. (And I agree with him that it is 
easier to do it in VBA than COBOL). Once you have the spreadsheet looking 
how you want it with the right properties set up on the right cells, SAVE it 
as a TEMPLATE.

Then you mail the template to your users and they import the .CSVs into it.

or C# somewhere that manipulates Excel. If I get a chance I'll try and find 
some code samples for you. This is just the kind of thing I would want to 
post to the web site. "How do I...?" stuff for COBOL.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: .CVS to .XLS

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-05T02:46:46+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<768denF1b9ne7U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49fcc158$0$23780$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <7648v7F1ak1i8U1@mid.individual.net> <49fd1fa2$0$23775$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <766qrkF1as6h8U1@mid.individual.net> <49fe8543$0$23756$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul H wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:766qrkF1as6h8U1@mid.individual.net...
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
> Pete.

OK, I took some time out from Web Development and looked at Excel... :-)

I'm running Excel 2007, so I can't guarantee the result on other versions.

The most difficult thing to decide is exactly WHICH properties need to be 
manipulated.

I opened the VB object browser in Excel and there are hundreds (thousands?) 
of classes and obects.

I ran some searches on things like "percentage" and "number format" etc... 
and found some things that looked promising, then I spent an entertaining 
hour trying to get them to work. Having no idea what values they will accept 
didn't help and I could see it was going nowhere.

Then it suddenly dawned on me...

"All ya gotta do is..."  record a macro that does what you want and the 
system will write the code for you!

I did this and, sure, enough, it wrote the VB code I needed.

Here's how you can see exactly what's going on...

Open a new blank Excel spreadsheet. Select the "Developer" tab and click 
"Visual Basic".  Paste the following into the right hand pane...


Sub Pete()

Dim objA1
Set objA1 = Sheet1.[A1]
objA1.Select
Selection.Style = "Percent"
Selection.NumberFormat = "0.00%"
objA1.Value = 0.1234

End Sub

Now press F8 to step through the code.

Here's the blow-by-blow:

Dim objA1......... allocates storage for what will be an object reference.

Set objA1 = Sheet1.[A1]..... creates an object instance of cell A1 in 
Sheet1. This gives us access to all the things the cell "knows"... its 
properties and methods.

objA1.Select ......invokes the cell's "select" behaviour. Cell A1 is now 
selected. This also creates the "Selection" object which we can reference, 
instead of objA1. You can think of the selection object as a "proxy" for the 
cell object. They are really one and the same in this instance.

Selection.Style = "Percent".....sets the "Style" property of the selection 
object to have the value "Percent" . Any number placed in the cell will be 
interpreted as a percentage.

Selection.NumberFormat = "0.00%".... This tells the selection object (via 
its "NumberFormat" property) how many decimals to shift the result by, and 
to put a percent sign on the right of it.

objA1.Value = 0.1234...... this puts the value "0.1234" as you used in your 
example, into the cell.

Now switch back to your Excel spreadsheet which should still be on the 
screen behind the VB stuff (there's a little Excel icon in the toolbar on 
the VB screen that will swap it back if it is minimised or you've lost it).

Look at cell A1.    It should be showing "12.34%".  Notice that the cell 
actually contains "0.1234".

Try entering other decimal values into the same cell and you will see that 
it does what you want.

So the two magic properties (for any cell you want to have this behaviour) 
are:

.Style  (has to be set to "Percentage"
.NumberFormat   has to be set to "0.00%"

NOW, you need to translate this into COBOL and amend the program that reads 
the .CSV accordingly. You said it has a number of invokes in it. From those, 
you should be able to see what objects are being used and what names have 
been given to them.

For example, you will need to see how the program references a given cell. 
As it is using Automation to "talk" to Excel it would be something like:


W-S
...
01  objSheet1   OBJECT REFERENCE  COM.
01  objA1         OBJECT REFERENCE  COM.

01  cell-A1    pic xx value "A1".
01  percent    pic x( 10) value "Percentage".
01  cellFormat  pic x(5) value "0.00%".
...

PD
...

invoke objSheet1  "cells"
           using  cell-A1
           returning objA1 *> gets an object reference to A1...
end-invoke

invoke objA1 "SET-Style"
           using  percent
end-invoke


invoke objA1 "SET-NumberFormat"
           using  cellFormat
end-invoke

I'm sorry these are Fujitsu examples using the Fujitsu *COM Class for 
Automation. This is a layer that simplifies using COM Automation. It will 
work differently under MF. You need to read the MF docs and find out how 
they use COM Automation.

Then, when you have it working, write up what you did and send me the code; 
I need some MF examples of COM interfacing for the cobol21 website... :-)

If this all looks terribly intimidating to you, visit the website and read 
about the Component Object Model  (COM) in the section on components. (It 
will be a day or two yet before I finish writing it).  It really isn't that 
hard.

http://primacomputing.co.nz/cobol21

Pete.
```

```


"James J. Gavan" <jgavandeletethis@shaw.ca> wrote in message 
news:TW1Nl.10721$CN5.245@newsfe23.iad...
> Paul H wrote:
>>>Per my post named "XLS, not .CSV from NETX?" dated Tuesday, March 24,
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Even if they are not doing so today, who knows what they will do tomorrow? 
The fact that they have requested the data asa spreadsheet implies they may 
havefurther uses for it.

> From above you are sending a COBOL file as a CSV to Excel. You are storing 
> the percentage in a COBOL file as pic v9(04); (that appears weird for a 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> Jimmy

I don't think so.

The obvious easy solution is sometimes not the best one. ("All ya gotta do 
is..." - Doc Dwarf)

No Jimmy, it is standard practice for percentages to be expressed as .99 or 
.9999 in applications which use spreadsheets. That's why Excel provides 
properties to "translate" the fraction into a formatted percentage on 
output.

Although you, quite properly, added the disclaimer about further processing, 
there is no guarantee that further processing will not occur, and even if it 
doesn't, you have now broken a convention as to how percentages are stored 
in spreadsheets.

If he did what you say, the target spreadsheet would have to contain a macro 
to "deformat" the display string so it could be used in further calculation. 
Otherwise, all the percentages would be wrong if the sheet is manipulated or 
passed on for any further processing. Breaking an accepted convention which 
then requires further processing before you can use it, solves an immediate 
short term problem, but it creates a longer term one, which is confusing for 
people who expect percentages to be recorded in accordance with convention.

Best avoided.

The Excel solution explained previously is not difficult and it means the 
cell retains the value it is expected to have; it just displays it in a 
"percent" format.

Maybe you could post some Micro Focus OO COBOL (Net Express?) that shows how 
to invoke these properties when using Excel via Automation?

That would be really useful...

I haven't been able to locate any Net Express documents that have samples of 
COM (Automation) processing. Do you know if there are examples in the docs 
that come with the package?

Pete.
```

Pete,

Again, I hope to be able to identify field types.

Since my program converts .CVS files into .XLS files, in order to add bold 
to the headings, and correctly deal with column sizes and features, it is 
preferable if the content of every column does not have to be pre-identified 
by the program that calls this one.

I can deal with special cases, like the percentage example now, but some 
fields are evidently self-identifying.  Data like 05/08/09 displays as 
05/08/2009 automatically - 2 bytes wider.  So if my program just measures 
the width of the input data, then sets the column width to that, it is short 
by 2 bytes.

Can I somehow ask each field, as I first encounter non-empty contents, 
sometimes many records into the file, what kind of field it is?  If not all 
types, then some would be a help.

Paul
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: .CVS to .XLS

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-10T01:12:53+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<76ldqmF1dcsd7U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<49fcc158$0$23780$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <7648v7F1ak1i8U1@mid.individual.net> <49fd1fa2$0$23775$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <766qrkF1as6h8U1@mid.individual.net> <49fe8543$0$23756$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <768denF1b9ne7U1@mid.individual.net> <4a046244$0$23771$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net> <TW1Nl.10721$CN5.245@newsfe23.iad> <76k0spF1d36niU1@mid.individual.net> <4a0519e1$0$23759$bbae4d71@news.suddenlink.net>`

```
Paul H wrote:
> Pete,
>
> Again, I hope to be able to identify field types.

Then you have a problem, Paul.

CSV files recognise 2 types: Numeric and Alpha.  Your Spreadsheet recognises 
a number of types, including percentage and date (as you discovered).

What, to the CSV file is a string,  say, "10/05/09"  on the spreadsheet is a 
string only if it is stored in a cell of type string. If you store it in a 
cell of type Date then it will display as a date using whichever date format 
is selected for the display of dates (it could just as well display on the 
spreadsheet as: "Saturday, 10th May, 2009")

>
> Since my program converts .CVS files into .XLS files, in order to add
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>
It doesn't, and attempting to do so would seem to be a pointless exercise.

Just load your CSV into the spreadsheet.  The TARGET determines the format.

Ah, but with a blank spreadsheet, you HAVE no target formats... That's why I 
suggested you use templates for this. Still, as you explained there are too 
many different formats to make that viable.

> I can deal with special cases, like the percentage example now,

Percentages are NOT a special case; they are a normal part of life in 
SpreadsheetWorld.


> but
> some fields are evidently self-identifying.

No, there are no "self-identifying" fields. What identifies the type of a 
field is the type of the cell it is being loaded into.

For example, if I have on the CSV file a field which is .1234. If I load it 
into the spread sheet the sheet will assume it is a numeric field and it 
will be loaded to a cell suitable for holding numbers with fractions. If I 
display the contents of the cell it will show as "0.1234" or whatever format 
string I have applied to it for zero suppression or whatever. Now, if I tell 
the spreadsheet that the SAME CELL is a percentage, and provide a percentage 
format string, it will display as "12.34%".  The contents of the actual cell 
are still .1234 and they never changed. (This is a little different form 
COBOL where we would move the filed to an editied picture... on the 
spreadsheet you can think of the spreadsheet moving the field to a work area 
where it is edited. Correctly, it "applies" the format string to the data, 
but does not change the data.

Again, if I have a string on the CSV file that is "10/05/09". This might be 
a part number, an account sales territory, or a myriad of things... it 
doesn't HAVE to be a date... You aply it to the spreadsheet and (unless it 
has been told differently) the spreadsheet cell stores it as a general 
string. It displays as "10/05/09". Now we tell the spreadsheet it is a date, 
and we select "long date" as the format for it. Suddenly it appears on the 
spreadsheet as "Saturday, 10th May, 2009". but the cell still contains 
"10/05/09". Change the format on the same cell to be "short date" and it 
displays as "10/05/2009", but the content is still "10/05/09". (That isn't 
strictly true because the actual content is a floating point number; but 
"conceptually" it retains the original string)

So you can see that the cell type and the format properties of the cell 
decide how the data renders on the spreadsheet.

It is also important to note that the spreadsheet validates data before 
storing it. If you try and put 29/02/2009 into a Date cell it will reject it 
(or change the cell type to be a general string so it can accommodate it, 
depending on how things are configured.) But there is no problem with 
loading this into a cell defined to hold a general string.

BOTTOM LINE: The target cell type determines how data will be displayed on 
the spreadsheet. As long as you put numbers into numeric cells, strings into 
string cells, percentages into percentage cells and dates into date cells, 
your spreadsheet will contain valid data. Usually, if the spreadsheet is 
blank it will try and accommodate whatever it encounters as either numeric 
or string. (You can hardly expect it to know psychically that a certain 
number is a percentage or a certain string is a date...:-))

>Data like 05/08/09
> displays as 05/08/2009 automatically - 2 bytes wider.

ONLY because it is being loaded into a Date cell and the display format is 
"short date"


>So if my
> program just measures the width of the input data, then sets the
> column width to that, it is short by 2 bytes.

Yep. See explanation above.

It could be short by a lot more, depending on the type of date specified.

>
> Can I somehow ask each field, as I first encounter non-empty contents,
> sometimes many records into the file, what kind of field it is?  If
> not all types, then some would be a help.

You can if you precede each field in the CSV file with a numeric code 
representing a data type, but there are some flaws with this idea...

1. It is unwieldy and inelegant.
2. Even if you KNOW the type of the input field, the spreadsheet will 
interpret it as the type of the TARGET cell, and completely ignore your 
input type.
3. It complicates the processing of the CSV file which is no longer 1:1 with 
the spreadsheet.

So, it doesn't help you to know the type of the source.

You could use a "Type mapping" for every spreadsheet. Again not an 
attractive proposition.

WHAT TO DO...?

So maybe you need to apply some "intelligence" to the CSV fields.

You can already identify NUMERIC or STRING pretty easily. Why not add some 
further refinement?

if it is numeric, check if it is a percentage.

if it is a string, check if it is a date...

You should get back from this something telling you: number, string, 
percent, date. Set the target cell properties appropriately, THEN move the 
data to it.

Personally, I'd write a component that takes a CSV string as input and 
returns the type as above. (It might be useful in other places...)

Up to you how you decide what is what, but half the battle is knowing what 
you are trying to do... :-)

HTH,

Pete.
```

## Thread: Power Cobol — 13 msgs

### Power Cobol

- **From:** "Jude" <jude_robert@cox.net>
- **Date:** 2009-05-04T19:50:26-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<M3MLl.11253$BZ3.1418@newsfe12.iad>`

```
Using Power Cobol on a Windows machine, how to check if a file is in use by 
a non Power Cobol program?

Thanks!
```

#### ↳ Re: Power Cobol

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz
- **Date:** 2009-05-04T21:23:13-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<be23a324-d46f-4e37-a93b-65a229137a27@i28g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<M3MLl.11253$BZ3.1418@newsfe12.iad>`

```
On May 5, 12:50 pm, "Jude" <jude_rob...@cox.net> wrote:
> Using Power Cobol on a Windows machine, how to check if a file is in use by
> a non Power Cobol program?
>
> Thanks!

You are not clear with your question.

"file is in use"

Is this 'file' a shared indexed file, or a line sequential file or
some other ?

Is that:
 a (non Power-Cobol) program?

Or:
 a (non-Power) Cobol program?

In the case of a shared indexed or relative file you probably can't
detect whether a file is being used by another Fujitsu Cobol program,
whether that is Power Cobol or not. You also probably can't tell
whether another program, in any language, is using it via the file
access callable routines.

In the case of non-shared files such as LINE SEQUENTIAL you will most
likely fail to open it but will not be able to determine whether the
other program is Cobol, Power Cobol or some other.

You also don't indicate the purpose of the check. If is it because you
don't want to use it while another program is using it then try
opening it exclusive. It will fail if another is using it.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Power Cobol

```
The Power Cobol program monitors a directory checking for new files for 
processing. I have no control over the process that copies the files to this 
folder. These are sequential text files, transaction files mostly, that can 
be many MB in size. I don't want to start processing a file until it has 
been completely copied.

The PC program checks the directory every minute and processes any new files 
then moves them to an archive location. Currently the program will save the 
size of a new file and then compare it to the size of the file a minute 
later. If the size is the same it is assumed that the file is complete and 
can be safely processed.

While this seems to be safe enough I'm still looking for a sure way of 
knowing that the file is complete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Power Cobol

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2009-05-05T14:16:09-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<eg0Ml.9797$im1.5778@nlpi061.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<M3MLl.11253$BZ3.1418@newsfe12.iad> <be23a324-d46f-4e37-a93b-65a229137a27@i28g2000prd.googlegroups.com> <6SVLl.37401$3k7.28781@newsfe17.iad>`

```
> The PC program checks the directory every minute and processes any new 
> files then moves them to an archive location. Currently the program will 
> save the size of a new file and then compare it to the size of the file a 
> minute later. If the size is the same it is assumed that the file is 
> complete and can be safely processed.

> While this seems to be safe enough I'm still looking for a sure way of 
> knowing that the file is complete.


Instead of clock, use  change notification objects to trgger your 
processing.  This flag:

      FILE_NOTIFY_CHANGE_LAST_WRITE Any change to the last write-time of 
files in the watched directory or subtree causes a change notification wait 
operation to return. The operating system detects a change to the last 
write-time only when the file is written to the disk. For operating systems 
that use extensive caching, detection occurs only when the cache is 
sufficiently flushed.


... should handle it.

When I've used these before, adding  new file to a folder results in 
multiple notifications, the trick is to use this one, since the others may 
occur before the file is completely copied or written.

Of course, you could always use your size/timestamp technique  AFTER you 
have received this notification...

  WaitForSingleObject
  GetSize/Timestamp of 'file of interest'
  DO
      WAIT some reasonable amount of time
     Check size/TimeStamp again. If no change it must be 'done'
END DO
```

Hey Jude (sorry I couldn’t resist).

Why don’t you just rename the file. If it is in use the rename will
fail and if it is not in use the file will change.
Example: if your files are named something like “Trx20090506edi.DAT”
rename *.dat *.DDD
then copy *.ddd to archive\*.dat and process any file that copies.

Good luck
Tom

Um, the standard change notification objects do not discriminate between 
"who" changed the file... even it it's yourself.

I have this in the EDI Pal(tm) software...  one of the user options for this 
editing program is "monitor file for external changes and report  Yes/No."

So I set that up... but when I am going to "save" myself, I disable the 
change notification object, save and restart the watch. Otherwise I get 
notified that 'someone' has changed the file.... even though that 'someone' 
was me.

Good way to get stuck in a loop. Trust me, it's a REALLY good way to get 
stuck in a loop........

> that 'someone' was me.

Yes, I use it for checking that files which are being edited in a certain 
environment are not being edited outside it. The point is I KNOW which files 
MY software is changing and I know what time I change them.

I think your approach is just as valid though it's a bit of a pain turning 
stuff on and off, and there is a possibility of getting a loop or someone 
changing it while you have monitoring disabled.

I was in a hurry when I responded to this and could have explained it 
better.

>
> Good way to get stuck in a loop. Trust me, it's a REALLY good way to
> get stuck in a loop........
>

Not the way I do it. At least, so far, I have never had a loop on it.

Pete
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Power Cobol

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Michael Mattias" <mmattias@talsystems.com>
- **Date:** 2009-05-06T08:17:32-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Y5gMl.28695$yr3.11475@nlpi068.nbdc.sbc.com>`
- **References:** `<M3MLl.11253$BZ3.1418@newsfe12.iad> <76a13bF1bqo6eU1@mid.individual.net> <Rk0Ml.9799$im1.8066@nlpi061.nbdc.sbc.com> <76bvniF1c9j6rU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:76bvniF1c9j6rU1@mid.individual.net...
> Michael Mattias wrote:
>> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> changing it while you have monitoring disabled.

If you do it in the right order this cannot happen:
User hits "save"
Open file for exclusive use (no can do? tell user!)
disable monitor
Write changed data to file (you do have exclusive use at this point)
Close file
Restart monitor

"In theory" someone could change the file between the close and the restart. 
I'll pay off if that happens.

Besides.... in this application, if you have more than one party editing the 
file at the same time you have bigger fish to fry anyway.

MCM
```

## Thread: Named pipe with MF Cobol — 3 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

### Named pipe with MF Cobol

- **From:** Vaclav Snajdr <vsn@snajdr.de>
- **Date:** 2009-05-05T11:52:22+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gtp2g9$n34$03$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
Hi folks,
there is no problem to work with named pipe in some languages, for example
tcl/tk (mkfifo -m600 VSFILE, 1.user open VSFILE w, flush "abcd", 2.user
open VSFILE r, gets, the content is "abcd").
I would like to realize the third user with Cobol. 
Has somebody did it?
Thanks. 
```

#### ↳ Re: Named pipe with MF Cobol

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>
- **Date:** 2009-05-05T11:51:42-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gtpri0012lm@news6.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<gtp2g9$n34$03$1@news.t-online.com>`
- **References:** `<gtp2g9$n34$03$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
Vaclav Snajdr wrote:
> Hi folks,
> there is no problem to work with named pipe in some languages, for example
> tcl/tk (mkfifo -m600 VSFILE, 1.user open VSFILE w, flush "abcd", 2.user
> open VSFILE r, gets, the content is "abcd").
> I would like to realize the third user with Cobol. 

What does this mysterious third user do? Your example only described
users 1 and 2.

> Has somebody did it?

Hard to say, since we don't know exactly what you want to do. You can
certainly read from a FIFO (Unix "named pipe") in Micro Focus COBOL on
Unix. Just define it as a line-sequential file. I believe there are
examples in the documentation.
```

#### ↳ Re: Named pipe with MF Cobol

- **From:** Vaclav Snajdr <vsn@snajdr.de>
- **Date:** 2009-05-07T10:02:55+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<gtu4r6$j6q$03$1@news.t-online.com>`
- **References:** `<gtp2g9$n34$03$1@news.t-online.com>`

```
the possible way is to declare a separate channel for reading (file INP)
and a separate channel for writing (file TOU). It is okay if you create  
can create the other side. But if the other side use only one channel
(with the possibility read and write), you have a problem.

cobol side

       IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
       PROGRAM-ID. PIPEX.
       AUTHOR. V.SNAJDR
       DATE-WRITTEN. 07/05/09.
      *  test mit fifo (named pipe) input output
       ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
       COPY "envi".
       INPUT-OUTPUT SECTION.
       FILE-CONTROL.
       SELECT INP  ASSIGN TO    DAZU-INP
                   ORGANIZATION LINE SEQUENTIAL,
                   ACCESS   IS  SEQUENTIAL,
                   STATUS   IS  D-STATUS
                   LOCK MODE IS MANUAL.
       SELECT TOU  ASSIGN TO    DAZU-TOU
                   ORGANIZATION LINE SEQUENTIAL,
                   ACCESS   IS  SEQUENTIAL,
                   STATUS   IS  D-STATUS
                   LOCK MODE IS MANUAL.

       DATA DIVISION.
       FILE SECTION.
       FD  INP  DATA RECORD INP-STAMM.
       01  INP-STAMM.
           03  INP-DATEN            PIC X(80).

       FD  TOU  DATA RECORD TOU-STAMM.
       01  TOU-STAMM.
           03  TOU-DATEN            PIC X(80).

       COPY "bmf/ws".
       COPY "wsi1".
       COPY "wsenea".
       01  DAZU-INP                 PIC X(70).
       01  DAZU-TOU                 PIC X(70).
       01  WAS                      PIC X.

       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
       HAUPTPROGRAMM SECTION.
       H10.
           MOVE "/data2/vs2/CfrT" TO DAZU-INP.  *> CobolFromTcl
           MOVE "/data2/vs2/CtoT" TO DAZU-TOU.  *> CobolToTcl
           DISPLAY SPACE
           DISPLAY "FIFO I-O"      AT 0101.
           DISPLAY  DAZU-INP       AT 0201.

           OPEN     INPUT          INP.
           DISPLAY  "OPENED"       AT 0260.
           display status-1        at 0270 with underline.
           display status-2        at 0273 with underline.
           DISPLAY  DAZU-TOU       AT 0301.

           OPEN     OUTPUT         TOU.
           DISPLAY  "OPENED"       AT 0360.
           display status-1        at 0370 with underline.
           display status-2        at 0373 with underline.
       H0.
           DISPLAY  "I)put O)utput E)nde"
                                   AT 0401.
           INITIALIZE              WAS TOU-DATEN INP-DATEN.
           ACCEPT   WAS            AT 0425 WITH AUTO.
           IF       WAS      =     "E" OR "e"   GO TO   PEND.
           IF       WAS      =     "I" OR "i"
                    PERFORM        MACHE-INPUT.
           IF       WAS      =     "O" OR "o"
                    PERFORM        MACHE-OUTPUT.
           GO TO                   H0.

       PEND.
           CLOSE                   INP TOU.
           EXIT                    PROGRAM.
           STOP                    RUN.

       MACHE-OUTPUT SECTION.
           DISPLAY "EINGABE:"      AT 1001.
           ACCEPT   TOU-DATEN      AT 1101.
           WRITE                   TOU-STAMM.

       MACHE-INPUT SECTION.
           DISPLAY "READ INPUT"    AT   0501.
           READ    INP             AT   END     EXIT    SECTION.
           display status-1        AT   0520.
           display status-2        AT   0523.
           DISPLAY "GELESEN:"      AT   0601.
           DISPLAY INP-DATEN       AT   0610 WITH SIZE 50.

tcl side

       #                                        pipex.tcl
       set f14     ariel-14
       set f12     ariel-12
       set t        " Test FIFO file VSF "
       labelframe  .lf  -text $t          -width   200 \
                        -font $f14        -height  100

       button      .lf.s     -text Start        -command do_start   -font
$f12
       button      .lf.i     -text Input        -command do_input   -font
$f12
       button      .lf.o     -text Output       -command do_output  -font
$f12
       label       .lf.l     -text "sendtime"                       -font
$f12
       label       .lf.r     -text "receive "                       -font
$f12
       button      .lf.e     -text End          -command do_ende    -font
$f12

       pack  .lf.s .lf.i .lf.o .lf.l .lf.r .lf.e -side left -padx 15
       pack        .lf


       proc do_output {} {
            set     a [expr [clock seconds]]
            puts  $::ou $a
            flush $::ou
            .lf.l configure -text "sended:$a" -bg green
            .lf.r configure                   -bg white
           }

       proc do_input {} {
            if {[gets $::in record] < 0} {
                return
             }
            .lf.r configure -text "received:$record" -bg green
            .lf.l configure                          -bg white
           }

       proc do_start {} {
            set ::ou [open CfrT w+]
       puts   "$::ou opened"
            set ::in [open CtoT r+]
       puts   "$::in opened"
            pack forget  .lf.s
       }

       proc do_ende {} {
            close $::ou
            close $::in
            exit
       }

#ende

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> 
> Maybe in NewsExpress it looks like that, but users of Agent see:

Just because one viewer renders MIME Multipart with HTML doesn't mean
they all do, or that they should.

> Blame yourself, not the poster.

Usenet was originally plain-text-only. There has never been any good
reason for anything other than plain text, and the rare shar or
uuencoded file, to be distributed by Usenet.

People who post MIME Multipart with HTML will be ignored by a
significant number of Usenet readers. Posters who want to reach the
full Usenet audience should follow Postel's interoperability
principle, and be conservative in what they generate. Doug's advice
was right on target.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ OT: HTML mail WAS: Re: Open-Cobol

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2009-05-09T12:42:18+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<76k1rcF1d9dceU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<yZgMl.22988$i24.19407@newsfe14.iad> <ngiMl.16136$pr6.3567@flpi149.ffdc.sbc.com> <8ov505tkr92p095m0bfo8ma2inji8udis2@4ax.com> <gu1jp70num@news1.newsguy.com>`

```


"Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
news:gu1jp70num@news1.newsguy.com...
> SkippyPB wrote:
>>
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> was right on target.

Hmmm... I don't dispute the history and, up until maybe a few years ago, I 
would have wholeheartedly agreed with you.

Today I can't.

When HTML format mail started becoming available (not in Usenet, but 
generally) many people were concerned about the possibility of javascript 
and similar scripting being embedded in it with malicious intent. I was one, 
and for a long time I would not accept or use anything except plain text.

But this meant that a world of useful effects was not available. Sure, 
adding pretty borders to mail is probably not worth the risk of a script 
infection, but things like different fonts and colour can improve mail out 
of sight.

I use a news server to post to this forum and they enforce plain mail only 
but I wish they didn't. :-)

The world is moving on. I've been using HTML mail for years now and never 
had a problem. Every post is scanned for any kind of scripting, in and out, 
and it seems to work pretty well. It is good to have formatting in a message 
retained and to be able to use colour to quickly identify various things.

Posting code samples in a non-proportional font, for example, is one 
valuable use for HTML in this forum.

I remember when colour 3270 screens first became available. Many of us (who 
had been used to green screens for years) thought it was an unnnecessary 
frippery...just meant more work with attribute bytes...) Within 3 months 
there were very few of us who wouldn't admit that colour in the user 
interface (even though it was still a text based interface) wasn't better.

Sometimes it takes a while for an idea to gel.

In the 21st century I, for one, would like to see this forum in HTML.

If I could post it, I would.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: HTML mail WAS: Re: Open-Cobol

- **From:** Michael Wojcik <mwojcik@newsguy.com>

- **Message-ID:** `<gu9kmc02cj0@news3.newsguy.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<76k1rcF1d9dceU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<yZgMl.22988$i24.19407@newsfe14.iad> <ngiMl.16136$pr6.3567@flpi149.ffdc.sbc.com> <8ov505tkr92p095m0bfo8ma2inji8udis2@4ax.com> <gu1jp70num@news1.newsguy.com> <76k1rcF1d9dceU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> "Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote in message 
> news:gu1jp70num@news1.newsguy.com...
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> of sight.

This is a matter of opinion, of course, but I don't agree. And this is
one of my areas of scholarship - I'm pursuing a degree in digital
rhetoric in my spare time, and I presented on the rhetorical use of
typography and color at a couple of academic conferences (Watson and
CCCC) in the last year. So this is a topic I have been studying in
some depth of late.

Something can only be improved in relation to a set of criteria for
success. Broadly speaking, the success criteria for written messages
have been grouped into three categories: communication (the domain of
technical communication scholars), persuasion (rhetoric), and
aesthetics (poetics).

Communication criteria consider the probability that the recipient's
interpretation is one intended by, or at least agreeable to, the
author; and also take up questions of efficiency, readability, etc.
Here textual effects can be used to good effect, but only with
significant caveats. The design has to be effective, and the
technology has to operate consistently. Neither of those are fulfilled
by the typical HTML email or Usenet post. Relatively few people have
any significant training in visual design, typography, readability,
etc. And because HTML user agents are not under the author's control,
significant care and expertise are required to ensure that the text
will render well in all, or even most, viewers. Most authors are
neither equipped for nor interested in satisfying those constraints.
Instead they use HTML text effects naively, in ways that add nothing
to or detract from the communicative effectiveness of their message.

The rhetorical functions of textual effects are not very well
understood (despite a fair amount of theory and empirical research on
the question). Authors are on even shakier ground trying to use
textual effects to make their messages more persuasive.

With email and Usenet, we can largely ignore questions of aesthetics.
There are few artists working in those media, and few readers
expecting to serve as that kind of audience.

All of these factors are aggravated when addressing a broad or unknown
audience - as is the case with Usenet. Many people still read Usenet
messages with text-only clients. Many people still read it over
low-bandwidth connections. Many people have visual requirements that
don't cope well with J Random Poster's lousy font choices - or just
don't have a compatible font installed. Many people simply don't want
to see HTML or MIME content. Those readers will be annoyed by attempts
to use textual effects. Already the author has alienated part of the
audience; it's unlikely that any communicative or rhetorical
advantages gained by textual effects will overcome that deficit.

> The world is moving on. I've been using HTML mail for years now and never 
> had a problem.

MIME proponents have been saying that since 1992. It's entirely beside
the point. A significant number of readers *do not want* MIME or HTML
in media where they add little or no value. We have not wanted them
since 1992. We are unlikely to spontaneously begin to want them in 2009.

Additional affordances do not automatically make a technology better.
Background music has recognized communicative, rhetorical, and
aesthetic value for television and film; but if I got an email with
embedded background music (assuming that content was processed by my
user agent, which is highly unlikely) I'd delete it unread. It'd be
just as annoying as those Hallmark cards that play music.

There are media where typographic and color effects can be used to
good purpose - though even then only with care. Email and Usenet are
rarely, if ever, among them.

Of course some will disagree. And they will post in HTML, and their
posts will be ignored by people who aren't using MIME-enabled user agents.
```

Perhaps they are artificial constraints? For normal emails, as long as they

Children learn to write using non-joined up letters, with a crayon. The 
results are naive and often not as effective as an offset lithopress would 
produce. But that doesn't mean they are worthless. As time goes by the 
writing becomes more fluid and the "practise" brings improvement. The same 
is true for use of HTML.
>
> The rhetorical functions of textual effects are not very well
> understood (despite a fair amount of theory and empirical research on
> the question). Authors are on even shakier ground trying to use
> textual effects to make their messages more persuasive.

The exact nature of nuclear physics is not clearly understood either (there 
is still argument about exactly what an electron is, never mind the many 
other particles involved), yet it desn't stop us building nuclear weapons or 
generating nuclear power. The point is that understanding doesn't HAVE to be 
immediately complete for something to be effective. Use it, and better 
understanding comes.

…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> to use textual effects.

So, you are saying if I want to communicate with someone who doesn't like 
HTML, I shouldn't use it.  I don't see much disagreement there. If I want to 
communicate with someone of a nervous disposition I shouldn't yell at them. 
All fine and good.

Now supposing I need to tell our HTML hater that he has just won Lotto and 
will never have to work a day in his life again and all the things he has 
yearned for and scrimped and saved for for years, are now within reach?

I send him an email which I want to include the Lotto Logo (immediate brand 
recognition) and I emphasise certain parts of the good news (like need to 
obtain qualified financial management, where and when he should attend to 
receive his cheque, etc.) with colour.

Do you seriously think that our Email hater is going to be "alienated" 
because he received the good news in HTML?

Our person of nervous disposition is about to be hit by an unobserved 
automobile. Should I gently whisper: "Don't be alarmed, but I think you need 
to relocate your person with some alacrity..." or just yell: "Look out!"?

The point of the above whimsies is that if the CONTENT of a message is 
sufficiently important, the FORMAT of it is subsumed.

If I make a post to Usenet that is of sufficient interest to people who use 
Usenet, they will read it whether it is in HTML or not. (OK, they won't at 
present because HTML is discouraged on Usenet and I CAN'T post to it in HTML 
unless I use a different, less convenient means than the one I have 
available. That's why we are having this conversation... :-))



> Already the author has alienated part of the
> audience;

It may well be a community of small minded, prissy,  reactionaries who 
detest change and whom I wouldn't want to reach anyway... point is, authors 
may not grieve over people dismissing their message because of the format, 
rather than the content.

> advantages gained by textual effects will overcome that deficit.

You are assuming that overcoming such a deficit is important to the author. 
It may be, or it may not be. Rather depends on thenature and  importance of 
the message.
>
>> The world is moving on. I've been using HTML mail for years now and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> the point.

 No it isn't. It is very much to the point.

"Don't use HTML, it's dangerous"

We use it for years, find that nothing happens, conclude that it ISN'T 
dangerous (provided proper precautions are taken), so the statement about it 
being so is incorrect. The point about not using it because of inherent 
danger is therfore defeated. Very much to the point of using or not using 
HTML.

>A significant number of readers *do not want* MIME or HTML
> in media where they add little or no value.

How do you know? it is certainly true that SOME readers prefer that HTML NOT 
be used, but you cannot know how "significant" a proportion that is. 
Furthermore, that proportion is diminishing. I know this from my own 
experience; I was once opposed to it and no longer am. It is unlikely that I 
am the only person on Usenet capable of changing my mind.

So, why should you impose that view on those of us who don't share it?

(If I post in HTML and some people don't read my stuff because of that, I 
have no problem with it at all. Their loss. Yet you are insisting I use 
plain text because YOU don't like HTML. Does that seem fair to you?)


>We have not wanted them
> since 1992. We are unlikely to spontaneously begin to want them in
> 2009.

You are speaking for yourself.  The "we" you employ is demonstrably 
unencompassing. People here have posted in HTML. Others here didn't like it, 
others again, think it is a good idea. It is controversial and arguable and 
that is why I am taking the time to respond to your post. Since 1992, I 
submit that a proportion of your "we" have moved out of that camp. It isn't 
a spontaneous change in 2009; it is a long experience suggesting that there 
is nothing to fear from HTML.
>
> Additional affordances do not automatically make a technology better.

No, not automatically. We need to look at them, evaluate them, and decide 
whether they are harmless or not.

> Background music has recognized communicative, rhetorical, and
> aesthetic value for television and film; but if I got an email with
> embedded background music (assuming that content was processed by my
> user agent, which is highly unlikely) I'd delete it unread.

And miss out on the Lotto prize?  :-)

I'd be intrigued as to why the sender chose to add music. It would get my 
attention. I had an email the other day from an old friend I hadn't heard 
from for some time. When I opened it, it was a voice message. Superb! I was 
still chuckling over it some time later when I managed to reach him on Skype 
video.

> It'd be
> just as annoying as those Hallmark cards that play music.
>
Whoa...Chill Dude... Don't like the music? Don't open the card :-)

> There are media where typographic and color effects can be used to
> good purpose - though even then only with care. Email and Usenet are
> rarely, if ever, among them.

A well expressed opinon which has nothing whatever to do with fact.

>
> Of course some will disagree. And they will post in HTML, and their
> posts will be ignored by people who aren't using MIME-enabled user
> agents.

Or, the people who ignore their posts will one day see a topic of particular 
interest and upgrade to an agent that gives them accessl. Then they will 
find that the CONTENT trumps the FORMAT every time.

An important message, badly formatted with garish colours and inappropriate 
fonts, is no less important because of the bad formatting.

Marshall MacLuhan tells us that the "media is the message" and he is correct 
for people who don't see beyond the medium. But media that were once strange 
and exotic (like email) are becoming woven into the fabric of our daily 
lives. People use HTML when communicating with their friends every day. It 
no longer has the "unexpectedness" that it once had and which MacLuhan's 
information theory tells us is required for data to have value. HTML is just 
an everyday part of the IT infrastructure. It is becoming "business as 
usual".

I see no reason why that shouldn't extend to Usenet.

Pete.

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> Michael Wojcik wrote:
>> Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> interested to see what you have to say.

Likewise.

>> Communication criteria consider the probability that the recipient's
>> interpretation is one intended by, or at least agreeable to, the
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> improved by use of HTML.

I think it's worth considering how you know that. Empirical
readability studies? Subjective (interviewing audience members) or
objective (comprehension tests, eye-tracking, etc)?

Research on readability is extensive, and one thing it has often shown
is that authors and audiences disagree on readability; and audience
subjective impressions of readability disagree with objective
measurements.

Of course there's more to reading than readability, as I noted; but
it's hard to demonstrate a simple relationship between textual effects
and "improving" a text, or even to define in general what an
unqualified "improvement" would be.

> And I can see that a number of mails I receive have 
> benefited from the use of HTML too.

I did write "the typical HTML email or Usenet post".

And, in retrospect, it was a mistake to conflate the two. Email and
Usenet are quite different media. Most emails go to a small set of
recipients chosen by the author, who may well have information about
their personal preferences, the conventions of that speech community,
and the technology available to them. That's not the case with Usenet,
where messages are distributed to a large number of recipients, mostly
unknown to the author.

> Use of colour for example makes the
> history of an interactive quoted mail immediately intelligble;

Not for visually-impaired users. Even simple limited color perception
often makes color cues useless and annoying - I speak from personal
experience.

> much better
> than the shabby chevrons we have to use here.

A subjective evaluation; I much prefer the chevrons.

> Who decides if the "design is effective"?

Again, there's a huge body of research on the question - it's not
something that can be summarized here (even if I had comprehensive
knowledge of it). But it's not the author (except insofar as the
author is also a member of the audience).

> If it communicates it can be argued to be effective.

We need a more granular metric than that to have a meaningful
discussion of authorial choices. (And "if it communicates" is itself a
vexed question, with all sorts of ongoing inquiries - communications
theory, communications research, various fields of linguistics,
philosophy of language, epistemology, etc - but that's a tangential
issue.)

> Basically, If you compare two mail samples where one
> has plain text and the other has HTML, then the one that communicates most 
> immediately, has to be the more effective.

I don't think any communication scholar would accept that as an
adequate rubric. I'm not sure what you mean by "most immediately"
there (fastest? with the least mediation? something else?), but there
are many other dimensions to communication effectivity: comprehension,
accuracy, specificity, retention, stimulation, appeal, reader effort, ...

Poetry and fiction often don't communicate "immediately", but that
doesn't stop them from being effective.

>> Relatively few people have
>> any significant training in visual design, typography, readability,
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> powerful arguments in this forum.

Relatively few people have training in physics, but they still fall
off things. :-)

Seriously: most people have at least informal training in rhetoric. We
learn argumentative techniques, what philosophers call "informal
logic" (a basic understanding of implication, contradiction, etc),
style, etc from our everyday experience of language use. Most people
have arguments every day (even if only internally) that they are
invested in. Consequently you don't need to study rhetoric in order to
make the occasional good argument.

Most people, on the other hand, take little notice of design in
general, much less specific areas like typography or color theory.

>> Instead they use HTML text effects naively, in ways that add nothing
>> to or detract from the communicative effectiveness of their message.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> is true for use of HTML.

I suspect in a thorough empirical study the data would not support
that thesis - that most HTML users gradually learn to use it
effectively or well. I've seen a lot of web pages.

Without such a study, we're both just guessing, though.

>> The rhetorical functions of textual effects are not very well
>> understood (despite a fair amount of theory and empirical research on
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> immediately complete for something to be effective.

No, but a lack of understanding certainly doesn't help.

> Use it, and better understanding comes.

Sometimes. Not, I think, for most people using HTML in email and the like.

>> With email and Usenet, we can largely ignore questions of aesthetics.
> 
> Ah, Michael, there are some of us who will NEVER ignore aesthetics in 
> ANYTHING... :-)

That was "questions of aesthetics" solely in relation to justifying
the use of HTML over plain text. Few emails or Usenet posts are
intentionally trying to create aesthetic effects through textual
effects; that sort of thing isn't even common in print, even in
avant-garde poetry.

More importantly, email and Usenet audiences typically don't view the
material as aesthetic objects (even if they're alert to aesthetic
questions of style and the like). Even if you could post your COBOL
question in a form like something from the Electronic Literature
Collection [1] to Usenet, it would likely be met with confusion and
disgruntlement. No one's looking to perform a physics textbook as an
opera, either.

>> There are few artists working in those media, and few readers
>> expecting to serve as that kind of audience.
> 
> Nevertheless, emails which are pleasing to the eye get more attention than 
> emails which are not.

Again, that claim needs to be backed up with empirical evidence. And
"pleasing to the eye" is again subjective. I'm always better-inclined
toward email I receive in plain text.

> Now supposing I need to tell our HTML hater that he has just won Lotto and 
> will never have to work a day in his life again and all the things he has 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> receive his cheque, etc.) with colour.

I would hope that no one would believe such an email, since it is
obviously a phishing expedition.

> Do you seriously think that our Email hater is going to be "alienated" 
> because he received the good news in HTML?

If it were me, and if for some unimaginable reason I didn't simply
delete the email as almost certainly bogus, I'd be annoyed by the
HTML, yes.

> The point of the above whimsies is that if the CONTENT of a message is 
> sufficiently important, the FORMAT of it is subsumed.

Content may well be more important than form in many circumstances.
But then either the form is irrelevant, in which case there's no need
to waste resources on HTML; or it's still relevant, in which case the
HTML hater will still be annoyed by it.

> If I make a post to Usenet that is of sufficient interest to people who use 
> Usenet, they will read it whether it is in HTML or not.

But my point - and the point of many other people who discourage the
use of HTML on Usenet - is that in fact we won't read it. (Actually,
if I'm using a MIME-multipart-aware user agent that's properly
configured, I may only see the plain-text version and be spared the
HTML entirely. But if I see the HTML, I'm ignoring the message.)

>> Already the author has alienated part of the audience;
> 
> It may well be a community of small minded, prissy,  reactionaries who 
> detest change and whom I wouldn't want to reach anyway...

Flattery will get you nowhere. :-)

And of course some people posting to Usenet may not care if some
readers ignore their messages. That strikes me as a suboptimal
approach if they're hoping to get the best answers that might be
available to them, but maybe they like to do things the hard way.

> point is, authors
> may not grieve over people dismissing their message because of the format, 
> rather than the content.

Sure. Foresight is not mandatory.

>> it's unlikely that any communicative or rhetorical
>> advantages gained by textual effects will overcome that deficit.
> 
> You are assuming that overcoming such a deficit is important to the author. 

That's a warrant for that line of argument, yes, but it's not my
operating assumption. People often write things for no good reason
whatsoever. Those who post to Usenet with no interest in the reception
of their message wouldn't care about this question anyway, so there's
no need to argue it for them.

>>> The world is moving on. I've been using HTML mail for years now and
>>> never had a problem.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>  No it isn't. It is very much to the point.

How so? Whether *you* have had a problem says very little about what
problems might exist for the many millions of other email users.

This is indeed the entire concept of interoperability: that it's not
about what works for you.

But in any case that was not my point. I don't even particularly care
about the security risks of HTML email, except insofar as they make it
easier for bot networks to grow and thereby annoy me with spam and
network congestion. I wrote above that it's beside the point because
the point is not the risks of HTML, but its *intended* use for
decoration of messages that neither need nor want it.

MIME was never a good use of technology. Email is a lousy way to
distribute anything other than short messages. There are better
vehicles for any other purpose.

> "Don't use HTML, it's dangerous"

And indeed it is, in various ways. Exploitable rendering bugs (there
are dozens documented). Cross-site scripting. Web-bug images.
Misleading links. Use of images and formatting to enhance social
engineering. All well-demonstrated security problems with HTML email.

> We use it for years, find that nothing happens,

That *you* don't notice the problems says nothing about whether they
exist.

But this is tangential, because the security problems are only icing
on this particular poisonous cake.

>> A significant number of readers *do not want* MIME or HTML
>> in media where they add little or no value.
> 
> How do you know? it is certainly true that SOME readers prefer that HTML NOT 
> be used, but you cannot know how "significant" a proportion that is. 

I wrote "a significant number", not "a significant proportion". I know
the former because this topic appears frequently and I've seen quite a
number of people voice similar objections to MIME and HTML. What would
constitute the latter is an open question.

> Furthermore, that proportion is diminishing. I know this from my own 
> experience; I was once opposed to it and no longer am.

And you know that in the meantime, two other people haven't formed the
opposite opinion?

> So, why should you impose that view on those of us who don't share it?

I fail to see how I'm imposing anything. I don't control Usenet.

> (If I post in HTML and some people don't read my stuff because of that, I 
> have no problem with it at all. Their loss. Yet you are insisting I use 
> plain text because YOU don't like HTML. Does that seem fair to you?)

I'm not insisting you do anything. I'm arguing against posting in
HTML. You may be persuaded, or you may not.


>> We have not wanted them
>> since 1992. We are unlikely to spontaneously begin to want them in
>> 2009.
> 
> You are speaking for yourself.

I'm speaking for myself and others who agree with me, as ought to have
been clear from context.

>  The "we" you employ is demonstrably unencompassing.

I never claimed otherwise.


>> Background music has recognized communicative, rhetorical, and
>> aesthetic value for television and film; but if I got an email with
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> And miss out on the Lotto prize?  :-)

Absolutely.

> Or, the people who ignore their posts will one day see a topic of particular 
> interest and upgrade to an agent that gives them accessl. Then they will 
> find that the CONTENT trumps the FORMAT every time.

The history of aesthetics suggest otherwise. Museum viewers flock to
paintings that represent nothing relevant to their experience.

> An important message, badly formatted with garish colours and inappropriate 
> fonts, is no less important because of the bad formatting.

But it may be less read. And, in fact, there are a great many
reception studies in visual rhetoric that document this very
phenomenon. As I recall, Jim Dubinski did a nice one on a NASA memo
about the space shuttle o-ring problem: the memo was distributed to
various relevant decision-makers prior to the Challenger explosion,
but went largely unread and didn't result in any action. Jim's study
showed this was largely due to the memo's form - all the relevant
content was present.

> Marshall MacLuhan tells us that the "media is the message" and he is correct 
> for people who don't see beyond the medium.

I don't know many people in rhetoric these days who think much of
McLuhan's popular writings, which are grossly oversimplified.

> It 
> no longer has the "unexpectedness" that it once had and which MacLuhan's 
> information theory tells us is required for data to have value.

That's not McLuhan's theory - that's basic information theory,
straight from Shannon. A message with no unexpectedness is a message
with no information. (The information content of a message is
inversely proportional to the probability of that message being
selected from the set of all possible messages for that communications
channel. If you can expect a given message with probability 1, then
the message has information content 0 - you already knew what it was
going to say.)

> It is becoming "business as usual".

Pete, I didn't expect to see you advocating business as usual! :-)


At any rate: my point in this entire discussion is that there are
people, such as myself and Doug Miller (and quite a number of others,
as a bit of research would show), who argue against the use of HTML in
Usenet and email. We cannot prevent people from using them - hence the
arguments. Others disagree - again, thus the arguments.

I've been using email for more than two decades, and Usenet for nearly
that long, and I've yet to see anything that would lead me to change
my position. And indeed the more I study relevant fields - digital
rhetoric and the like - the more I feel that position is justified.

Now, you may be correct that 1) on average, people are getting better
at using HTML, and 2) fewer people are opposed to its use in these
venues. That has not been my observation, and I haven't seen any data
to support it; but then I haven't done any sustained research into the
question either.

I will note that academia is in general getting better at training
students in new media, and consequently we can expect more HTML
authors with more-sophisticated understanding of the issues over time.
Hopefully that will improve the average quality of the web. Perhaps it
will do the same for HTML in Usenet and email - but in my book that's
still better bicycles for fish.


[1] http://collection.eliterature.org
```
